</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Family of aquatic flightless birds</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Penguin_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Penguin (disambiguation)">Penguin (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the original bird called "penguin", see <a href="/wiki/Great_Auk" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Auk">Great Auk</a>.</div>

data-file-height="1704" /></a></span></div><div class="text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/Snares_penguin" title="Snares penguin">Snares</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:107px;max-width:107px"><div style="height:115px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:African_penguin_side_profile.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/African_penguin_side_profile.jpg/120px-African_penguin_side_profile.jpg" decoding="async" width="105" height="115" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/African_penguin_side_profile.jpg/250px-African_penguin_side_profile.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3153" data-file-height="3456" /></a></span></div><div class="text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/African_penguin" title="African penguin">African</a></div></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>

</td></tr>












</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1289143401">@media(max-width:640px){body:not(.skin-minerva) .mw-parser-output .infobox{width:100%!important}body:not(.skin-minerva) .mw-parser-output .infobox th{width:50%}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:transparent}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}.mw-parser-output .infobox.biota .taxobox-edit-taxonomy img{background:transparent!important}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox{margin-top:0.5em}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox tr.taxonrow2 td{padding:2px 10px}</style>
<p><b>Penguins</b> are a group of <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">flightless</a> semi-aquatic <a href="/wiki/Sea_bird" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea bird">sea birds</a> which live almost exclusively in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>. Only one <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Galapagos_penguin" title="Galapagos penguin">Galapagos penguin</a>, lives at, and slightly north of, the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. Highly adapted for life in the ocean water, penguins have <a href="/wiki/Countershading" title="Countershading">countershaded</a> dark and white <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> and flippers for swimming. Most penguins feed on <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a>, fish, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a> and other forms of <a href="/wiki/Sea_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea life">sea life</a> which they catch with their bills and swallow whole while swimming. A penguin has a spiny tongue and powerful jaws to grip slippery prey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>They spend about half of their lives on land and the other half in the sea. The largest living species is the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguin</a> (<i>Aptenodytes forsteri</i>):<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> on average, adults are about 1.1&#160;m (3&#160;ft 7&#160;in) tall and weigh 35&#160;kg (77&#160;lb). The smallest penguin species is the <a href="/wiki/Little_penguin" title="Little penguin">little blue penguin</a> (<i>Eudyptula minor</i>), also known as the fairy penguin, which stands around 30–33&#160;cm (12–13&#160;in) tall and weighs 1.2–1.3&#160;kg (2.6–2.9&#160;lb).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pengsent&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pengsent-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today, larger penguins generally inhabit colder regions, and smaller penguins inhabit regions with temperate or <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical climates</a>. Some <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric" class="mw-redirect" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> penguin species were enormous: as tall or heavy as an adult human.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There was a great diversity of species in <a href="/wiki/Subantarctic" title="Subantarctic">subantarctic</a> regions, and <a href="/wiki/Palaeeudyptes_klekowskii" title="Palaeeudyptes klekowskii">at least one giant species</a> in a region around 2,000&#160;km south of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> 35&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>, during the Late <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, a climate decidedly warmer than today.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Auk_(Pinguinis_impennis)_specimen,_Kelvingrove,_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1108249.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Great_Auk_%28Pinguinis_impennis%29_specimen%2C_Kelvingrove%2C_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1108249.jpg/250px-Great_Auk_%28Pinguinis_impennis%29_specimen%2C_Kelvingrove%2C_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1108249.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="225" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Great_Auk_%28Pinguinis_impennis%29_specimen%2C_Kelvingrove%2C_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1108249.jpg/330px-Great_Auk_%28Pinguinis_impennis%29_specimen%2C_Kelvingrove%2C_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1108249.jpg 2x" data-file-width="426" data-file-height="640" /></a><figcaption>The name penguin was first used in the late 16th century for the great auk (pictured here) and was later applied to Southern Hemisphere birds due to their resemblance, though they are not closely related.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gaskell&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskell-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The word <i>penguin</i> first appears in literature at the end of the 16th century as a synonym for the <a href="/wiki/Great_auk" title="Great auk">great auk</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fuller&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When European explorers discovered what are today known as penguins in the Southern Hemisphere, they noticed their <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">similar appearance</a> to the great auk of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> and named them after this bird, although they are not closely related.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crofford10&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crofford10-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The etymology of the word <i>penguin</i> is still debated. The English word is not apparently of French,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CNRTL&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNRTL-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Online&#95;Etymology&#95;Dictionary&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Online_Etymology_Dictionary-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or Spanish<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DRAE&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRAE-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> origin (the latter two are attributed to the French word <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">pingouin</i></span>), but first appears in English or Dutch.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CNRTL&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNRTL-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some dictionaries suggest a derivation from <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">pen</i></span>, 'head' and <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">gwyn</i></span>, 'white',<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> including the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/American_Heritage_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="American Heritage Dictionary">American Heritage Dictionary</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AHD&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHD-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the <i><a href="/wiki/Century_Dictionary" title="Century Dictionary">Century Dictionary</a></i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-AHD&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHD-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster" title="Merriam-Webster">Merriam-Webster</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MW&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> on the basis that the name was originally applied to the great auk, either because it was found on <a href="/wiki/White_Head_Island" title="White Head Island">White Head Island</a> (<a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>: <i lang="cy">Pen Gwyn</i>) in Newfoundland, or because it had white circles around its eyes (though the head was black). However, the Welsh word <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">pen</i></span> can also be used to mean 'end' and, in a maritime context, <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">pen blaen</i></span> means 'front end or part, bow (of a ship), prow'.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative etymology links the word to <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">pinguis</i></span>, which means 'fat' or 'oil'.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Support for this etymology can be found in the alternative Germanic word for penguin, <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">fettgans</i></span> or 'fat-goose', and the related Dutch word <span title="Dutch-language text"><i lang="nl">vetgans</i></span>.
</p><p>A group of penguins in the water is sometimes called a <i>raft</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pinguinus">Pinguinus</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_auk" title="Great auk">Great auk</a></div>
<p>Since 1791, the Latin word <i>Pinguinus</i> has been used in <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">scientific classification</a> to name the genus of the <a href="/wiki/Great_auk" title="Great auk">great auk</a> (<i>Pinguinus impennis</i>, meaning "plump or fat without <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">flight feathers</a>"),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gaskell&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskell-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which became <a href="/wiki/Bird_extinction" title="Bird extinction">extinct</a> in the mid-19th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fuller&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As confirmed by a 2004 genetic study, the genus <i>Pinguinus</i> belongs in the family of the <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">auks</a> (Alcidae), within the order of the <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">Charadriiformes</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Johnsgard&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kuhletal2020&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhletal2020-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The birds currently known as penguins were discovered later and were so named by sailors because of their physical resemblance to the great auk. Despite this resemblance, however, they are not auks, and are not closely related to the great auk.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crofford10&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crofford10-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gaskell&#95;9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskell-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They do not belong in the genus <i>Pinguinus</i>, and are not classified in the same family and order as the great auk. They were classified in 1831 by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucien_Bonaparte" title="Charles Lucien Bonaparte">Charles Lucien Bonaparte</a> in several distinct genera within the family <a href="/wiki/Spheniscidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Spheniscidae">Spheniscidae</a> and order <a href="/wiki/Sphenisciformes" class="mw-redirect" title="Sphenisciformes">Sphenisciformes</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Systematics_and_evolution">Systematics and evolution</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_penguins" title="List of penguins">List of penguins</a></div>
<p>The family name of Spheniscidae was given by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucien_Bonaparte" title="Charles Lucien Bonaparte">Charles Lucien Bonaparte</a> from the genus <a href="/wiki/Spheniscus" class="mw-redirect" title="Spheniscus">Spheniscus</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the name of that genus comes from the Greek word <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">σφήν</span></span> <i>sphēn</i> "<a href="/wiki/Wedge" title="Wedge">wedge</a>" used for the shape of an <a href="/wiki/African_penguin" title="African penguin">African penguin</a>'s swimming flippers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some recent sources<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ksepka&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ksepka-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Clarke&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> apply the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_taxonomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogenetic taxonomy">phylogenetic taxon</a> Spheniscidae to what here is referred to as Spheniscinae. Furthermore, they restrict the phylogenetic taxon Sphenisciformes to flightless taxa, and establish the phylogenetic taxon Pansphenisciformes as equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Linnean_taxonomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Linnean taxonomy">Linnean taxon</a> Sphenisciformes,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Clarke&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> i.e., including any flying basal "proto-penguins" to be discovered eventually. Given that neither the relationships of the penguin subfamilies to each other nor the placement of the penguins in the avian <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> is presently resolved, this is confusing, so the established Linnean system is followed here.
</p><p>The number of penguin species is typically listed as between seventeen and nineteen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Ornithologists%27_Union" title="International Ornithologists&#39; Union">International Ornithologists' Union</a> recognizes six genera and eighteen species:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Penguin genera
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Genus</th>
<th>Species</th>
<th>Image of <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type species</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><i><a href="/wiki/Eudyptes" class="mw-redirect" title="Eudyptes">Eudyptes</a></i> (crested penguins)</td>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Evolution">Evolution</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_11266_Penguin_tracks.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/CSIRO_ScienceImage_11266_Penguin_tracks.jpg/250px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_11266_Penguin_tracks.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/CSIRO_ScienceImage_11266_Penguin_tracks.jpg/500px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_11266_Penguin_tracks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2657" data-file-height="1993" /></a><figcaption>Penguin tracks in the sand on <a href="/wiki/Bruny_Island" title="Bruny Island">Bruny Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Although the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeographic</a> history of Sphenisciformes is well-researched, many prehistoric forms are not <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy#Alpha_and_beta_taxonomy" title="Taxonomy">fully described</a>.  Some seminal articles about the evolutionary history of penguins have been published since 2005.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ksepka&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ksepka-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-B&amp;G&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B&amp;G-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Slack&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slack-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (October 2024)">excessive citations</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> penguins lived around the time of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> in the general area of southern New Zealand and <a href="/wiki/Byrd_Land" class="mw-redirect" title="Byrd Land">Byrd Land</a>, Antarctica.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ksepka&#95;3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ksepka-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>, these areas were at that time less than 1,500 kilometres (930&#160;mi) apart rather than 4,000 kilometres (2,500&#160;mi). The <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a> of penguins and <a href="/wiki/Procellariiformes" title="Procellariiformes">Procellariiformes</a> can be roughly dated to the <a href="/wiki/Campanian" title="Campanian">Campanian</a>–<a href="/wiki/Maastrichtian" title="Maastrichtian">Maastrichtian</a> boundary, around 70–68 mya.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Slack&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slack-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Basal_fossils">Basal fossils</h4></div>
<p>The oldest known <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> penguin species are known from the <a href="/wiki/Waipara_Greensand" title="Waipara Greensand">Waipara Greensand</a> of New Zealand, which spans the late <a href="/wiki/Danian" title="Danian">Danian</a> to early <a href="/wiki/Thanetian" title="Thanetian">Thanetian</a> stages (~62.5–58 Ma) of the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> epoch. Several genera have been named from these outcrops, including <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeodyptes" title="Archaeodyptes">Archaeodyptes</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Daniadyptes" title="Daniadyptes">Daniadyptes</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Muriwaimanu" title="Muriwaimanu">Muriwaimanu</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sequiwaimanu" title="Sequiwaimanu">Sequiwaimanu</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Waimanu" title="Waimanu">Waimanu</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Waimanutaha" title="Waimanutaha">Waimanutaha</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Waiparadyptes" title="Waiparadyptes">Waiparadyptes</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WaiparaPenguins&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WaiparaPenguins-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While they were not as well-adapted to aquatic life as modern penguins, they were still flightless, with short wings adapted for deep diving.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Slack&#95;32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slack-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They swam on the surface using mainly their feet, but the wings were—as opposed to most other diving birds (both living and extinct)—already adapting to underwater locomotion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Perudyptes" title="Perudyptes">Perudyptes</a></i> from northern Peru was dated to 42 mya. An unnamed fossil from Argentina proves that, by the <a href="/wiki/Bartonian" title="Bartonian">Bartonian</a> (Middle Eocene), some 39–38 mya,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> primitive penguins had spread to South America and were in the process of expanding into <a href="/wiki/Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> waters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Clarke&#95;27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Palaeeudyptines">Palaeeudyptines</h4></div>
<p>During the Late Eocene and the Early <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> (40–30 mya), some lineages of gigantic penguins existed. <a href="/wiki/Nordenskjoeld%27s_giant_penguin" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordenskjoeld&#39;s giant penguin">Nordenskjoeld's giant penguin</a> was the tallest, growing nearly 1.80 meters (5.9 feet) tall. The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_giant_penguin" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealand giant penguin">New Zealand giant penguin</a> was probably the heaviest, weighing 80 kilograms (180&#160;lb) or more. Both were found on New Zealand, the former also in the Antarctic farther eastwards.
</p><p>Traditionally, most extinct species of penguins, giant or small, had been placed in the <a href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> <a href="/wiki/Subfamily" title="Subfamily">subfamily</a> called <a href="/wiki/Palaeeudyptinae" title="Palaeeudyptinae">Palaeeudyptinae</a>. More recently, with new taxa being discovered and placed in the <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> if possible, it is becoming accepted that there were at least two major extinct lineages. One or two closely related ones occurred in <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a>, and at least one other—which is or includes the paleeudyptines as recognized today – occurred on most <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subantarctic" title="Subantarctic">Subantarctic</a> coasts.
</p><p>Size plasticity was significant at this initial stage of <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">radiation</a>: on <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Island" title="Seymour Island">Seymour Island</a>, Antarctica, for example, around 10 known species of penguins ranging in size from medium to large apparently coexisted some 35 mya during the <a href="/wiki/Priabonian" title="Priabonian">Priabonian</a> (Late Eocene).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jadwiszczak&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jadwiszczak-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is not known whether the palaeeudyptines constitute a <a href="/wiki/Monophyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> lineage, or whether gigantism was evolved independently in a restricted Palaeeudyptinae and the Anthropornithinae – whether they were considered valid, or whether there was a wide size range present in the Palaeeudyptinae as delimited (i.e., including <i><a href="/wiki/Anthropornis_nordenskjoeldi" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropornis nordenskjoeldi">Anthropornis nordenskjoeldi</a></i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ksepka&#95;3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ksepka-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The oldest well-described giant penguin, the 5-foot (1.5&#160;m)-tall <i><a href="/wiki/Icadyptes_salasi" class="mw-redirect" title="Icadyptes salasi">Icadyptes salasi</a></i>, existed as far north as northern Peru about 36 mya.
</p><p>Gigantic penguins had disappeared by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a>, around 25 mya. Their decline and disappearance coincided with the spread of the <a href="/wiki/Squalodontidae" title="Squalodontidae">Squalodontidae</a> and other primitive, fish-eating <a href="/wiki/Toothed_whale" title="Toothed whale">toothed whales</a>, which competed with them for food and were ultimately more successful.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A new lineage, the <a href="/wiki/Paraptenodytes" title="Paraptenodytes">Paraptenodytes</a>, which includes smaller and stout-legged forms, had already arisen in southernmost South America by that time. The early <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> saw the emergence of another morphotype in the same area, the similarly sized but more gracile <a href="/wiki/Palaeospheniscinae" class="mw-redirect" title="Palaeospheniscinae">Palaeospheniscinae</a>, as well as the radiation that gave rise to the current <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> of penguins.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Origin_and_systematics_of_modern_penguins">Origin and systematics of modern penguins</h4></div>
<p>Modern penguins constitute two undisputed <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> and another two more basal genera with more ambiguous relationships.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-B&amp;G&#95;31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B&amp;G-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To help resolve the evolution of this <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a>, 19 high-coverage genomes that, together with two previously published genomes, encompass all extant penguin species have been sequenced.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The origin of the Spheniscinae lies probably in the latest Paleogene and, geographically, it must have been much the same as the general area in which the order evolved: the oceans between the Australia-New Zealand region and the Antarctic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Presumably diverging from other penguins around 40 mya,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it seems that the Spheniscinae were for quite some time limited to their ancestral area, as the well-researched deposits of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">Antarctic Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> have not yielded Paleogene fossils of the subfamily. Also, the earliest spheniscine lineages are those with the most southern distribution.
</p><p>The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aptenodytes" title="Aptenodytes">Aptenodytes</a></i> appears to be the basalmost divergence among living penguins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ksepka&#95;3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ksepka-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-CB08&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CB08-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They have bright yellow-orange neck, breast, and bill patches; incubate by placing their eggs on their feet, and when they hatch the chicks are almost naked. This genus has a distribution centred on the Antarctic coasts and barely extends to some Subantarctic islands today.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Pygoscelis" title="Pygoscelis">Pygoscelis</a></i> contains species with a fairly simple black-and-white head pattern; their distribution is intermediate, centred on Antarctic coasts but extending somewhat northwards from there. In external <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a>, these apparently still resemble the common ancestor of the Spheniscinae, as <i>Aptenodytes</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span> <a href="/wiki/Autapomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Autapomorph">autapomorphies</a> are, in most cases, fairly pronounced <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptation (biology)">adaptations</a> related to that genus' extreme <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> conditions. As the former genus, <i>Pygoscelis</i> seems to have diverged during the Bartonian,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but the range expansion and radiation that led to the present-day diversity probably did not occur until much later; around the <a href="/wiki/Burdigalian" title="Burdigalian">Burdigalian</a> stage of the Early <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>, roughly 20–15 mya.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Spheniscus" class="mw-redirect" title="Spheniscus">Spheniscus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Eudyptula" title="Eudyptula">Eudyptula</a></i> contain species with a mostly Subantarctic distribution centred on South America; some, however, range quite far northwards. They all lack <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoid</a> colouration and the former genus has a conspicuous banded head pattern; they are unique among living penguins by nesting in burrows. This group probably radiated eastwards with the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">Antarctic Circumpolar Current</a> out of the ancestral range of modern penguins throughout the <a href="/wiki/Chattian" title="Chattian">Chattian</a> (Late Oligocene), starting approximately 28 mya.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While the two genera separated during this time, the present-day diversity is the result of a <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> radiation, taking place some 4–2 mya.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Megadyptes</i>–<i>Eudyptes</i> clade occurs at similar <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a> (though not as far north as the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_penguin" class="mw-redirect" title="Galápagos penguin">Galápagos penguin</a>), has its highest diversity in the New Zealand region, and represents a westward dispersal. They are characterized by hairy yellow ornamental head feathers; their bills are at least partly red. These two genera diverged apparently in the Middle Miocene (<a href="/wiki/Langhian" title="Langhian">Langhian</a>, roughly 15–14 mya), although the living species of <i>Eudyptes</i> are the product of a later radiation, stretching from about the late <a href="/wiki/Tortonian" title="Tortonian">Tortonian</a> (Late Miocene, 8 mya) to the end of the Pliocene.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Geography">Geography</h4></div>
<p>The geographical and temporal pattern of spheniscine evolution corresponds closely to two episodes of <a href="/wiki/Global_cooling" title="Global cooling">global cooling</a> documented in the <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">paleoclimatic record</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The emergence of the Subantarctic lineage at the end of the Bartonian corresponds with the onset of the slow period of cooling that eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">ice ages</a> some 35 million years later. With habitat on the Antarctic coasts declining, by the Priabonian more hospitable conditions for most penguins existed in the Subantarctic regions rather than in Antarctica itself.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker,&#95;A.,&#95;Pereira,&#95;SL,&#95;Haddrath,&#95;OP,&#95;Edge,&#95;KA&#95;2006&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker,_A.,_Pereira,_SL,_Haddrath,_OP,_Edge,_KA_2006-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Notably, the cold Antarctic Circumpolar Current also started as a continuous circumpolar flow only around 30 mya, on the one hand forcing the Antarctic cooling, and on the other facilitating the eastward expansion of <i><a href="/wiki/Spheniscus" class="mw-redirect" title="Spheniscus">Spheniscus</a></i> to South America and eventually beyond.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite this, there is no fossil evidence to support the idea of crown radiation from the Antarctic continent in the Paleogene, although DNA study favors such a radiation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker,&#95;A.,&#95;Pereira,&#95;SL,&#95;Haddrath,&#95;OP,&#95;Edge,&#95;KA&#95;2006&#95;41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker,_A.,_Pereira,_SL,_Haddrath,_OP,_Edge,_KA_2006-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Later, an interspersed period of slight warming was ended by the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Miocene_Climate_Transition" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Miocene Climate Transition">Middle Miocene Climate Transition</a>, a sharp drop in global average temperature from 14 to 12 mya, and similar abrupt cooling events followed at 8 mya and 4 mya; by the end of the Tortonian, the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a> was already much like today in volume and extent. The emergence of most of today's Subantarctic penguin species almost certainly was caused by this sequence of Neogene climate shifts.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Relationship_to_other_bird_orders">Relationship to other bird orders</h4></div>
<p>Penguin ancestry beyond <i><a href="/wiki/Waimanu" title="Waimanu">Waimanu</a></i> remains unknown and not well-resolved by molecular or morphological analyses. The latter tend to be confounded by the strong adaptive <a href="/wiki/Autapomorphy" title="Autapomorphy">autapomorphies</a> of the Sphenisciformes; a sometimes perceived fairly close relationship between penguins and <a href="/wiki/Grebe" title="Grebe">grebes</a> is almost certainly an error based on both groups' strong diving adaptations, which are <a href="/wiki/Homoplasies" class="mw-redirect" title="Homoplasies">homoplasies</a>. On the other hand, different <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA sequence">DNA sequence</a> datasets do not agree in detail with each other either.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Gal%C3%A1pagos_penguin_(Spheniscus_mendiculus)_male.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gal%C3%A1pagos_penguin_%28Spheniscus_mendiculus%29_male.jpg/250px-Gal%C3%A1pagos_penguin_%28Spheniscus_mendiculus%29_male.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Gal%C3%A1pagos_penguin_%28Spheniscus_mendiculus%29_male.jpg/500px-Gal%C3%A1pagos_penguin_%28Spheniscus_mendiculus%29_male.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2430" data-file-height="1620" /></a><figcaption>The endangered Galapagos penguin. The penguin is an accomplished swimmer, having <a href="/wiki/Flipper_(anatomy)" title="Flipper (anatomy)">flippers</a> instead of wings.</figcaption></figure>
<p>What seems clear is that penguins belong to a clade of <a href="/wiki/Neoaves" title="Neoaves">Neoaves</a> (living birds except for <a href="/wiki/Paleognath" class="mw-redirect" title="Paleognath">paleognaths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fowl" title="Fowl">fowl</a>) that comprises what is sometimes called "<a href="/wiki/Aequornithes" title="Aequornithes">higher waterbirds</a>" to distinguish them from the more ancient <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>. This group contains such birds as <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">storks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rallidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Rallidae">rails</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>, with the possible exception of the <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">Charadriiformes</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Inside this group, penguin relationships are far less clear. Depending on the analysis and dataset, a close relationship to <a href="/wiki/Ciconiiformes" class="mw-redirect" title="Ciconiiformes">Ciconiiformes</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Slack&#95;32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slack-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or to <a href="/wiki/Procellariiformes" title="Procellariiformes">Procellariiformes</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Baker&#95;30-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> has been suggested. Some think the penguin-like <a href="/wiki/Plotopteridae" title="Plotopteridae">plotopterids</a> (usually considered relatives of <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anhinga" title="Anhinga">anhingas</a>) may actually be a sister group of the penguins and those penguins may have ultimately shared a common ancestor with the <a href="/wiki/Pelecaniformes" title="Pelecaniformes">Pelecaniformes</a> and consequently would have to be included in that order, or that the plotopterids were not as close to other pelecaniforms as generally assumed, which would necessitate splitting the traditional Pelecaniformes into three.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 analysis of whole genomes of 48 representative bird species has concluded that penguins are the <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a> of Procellariiformes,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jarvis2014&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jarvis2014-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> from which they diverged about 60 million years ago (95% CI, 56.8–62.7).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-LiC2014&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiC2014-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The distantly related Charadriiform <a href="/wiki/Puffin" title="Puffin">puffins</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kuhletal2020&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhletal2020-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which live in the North Pacific and North Atlantic, developed similar characteristics to survive in the Arctic and sub-Arctic environments. Like the penguins, puffins have a white chest, black back and short stubby wings providing excellent swimming ability in icy water. But, unlike penguins, puffins can fly, as flightless birds would not survive alongside land-based predators such as polar bears and foxes; there are no such predators in the Antarctic. Their similarities indicate that similar environments in different parts of the world can result in similar evolutionary developments, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Puffins are auks (Alcidae), akin to the aforementioned great auk, which have a greater resemblance to "true" penguins after converging on more features of their anatomy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Penguin_Wing.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Penguin_Wing.svg/250px-Penguin_Wing.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="391" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Penguin_Wing.svg/500px-Penguin_Wing.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="801" /></a><figcaption>Penguin wings have the same general bone structure as flighted birds, but the bones are shorter and stouter to allow them to serve as fins. 1). Humerus 2). Sesamoid Bone 3). Radius 4). Ulna 5). Radial Carpal bone 6). Carpometacarpus 7). Phalanges</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Taxidermy_of_a_penguin_skin.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Taxidermy_of_a_penguin_skin.jpg/250px-Taxidermy_of_a_penguin_skin.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="373" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Taxidermy_of_a_penguin_skin.jpg/500px-Taxidermy_of_a_penguin_skin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3872" /></a><figcaption>Taxidermized penguin skin</figcaption></figure>
<p>Penguins are superbly adapted to <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">aquatic</a> life. Their <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">wings</a> have evolved to become flippers, useless for flight in the air. In the water, however, penguins are astonishingly agile. Penguins' swimming looks very similar to birds' flight in the air.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Questionable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (November 2025)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Within the smooth <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> a layer of air is preserved, ensuring buoyancy. The air layer also helps insulate the birds in cold waters. On land, penguins use their <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tails</a> and wings to maintain balance for their upright stance.
</p><p>All penguins are <a href="/wiki/Countershading" title="Countershading">countershaded</a> for <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a> – that is, they have black backs and <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">wings</a> with white fronts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A predator looking up from below (such as an <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">leopard seal</a>) has difficulty distinguishing between a white penguin belly and the reflective water surface. The dark plumage on their backs camouflages them from above.
</p><p>Gentoo penguins are the fastest underwater birds in the world. They are capable of reaching speeds up to 36&#160;km (about 22 miles) per hour while searching for food or escaping from predators. They are also able to dive to depths of 170–200 meters (about 560–660 feet).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The small penguins do not usually dive deep; they catch their prey near the surface in dives that normally last only one or two minutes. Larger penguins can dive deep in case of need. Emperor penguins are the world's deepest-diving birds. They can dive to depths of approximately 550 meters (1,800 feet) while searching for food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Penguins either waddle on their feet or slide on their bellies across the snow while using their feet to propel and steer themselves, a movement called "tobogganing", which conserves energy while moving quickly. They also jump with both feet together if they want to move more quickly or cross steep or rocky terrain.
</p><p>Penguins have an average sense of <a href="/wiki/Hearing_(sense)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hearing (sense)">hearing</a> for birds;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wever&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wever-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> this is used by parents and chicks to locate one another in crowded <a href="/wiki/Seabird_colony" class="mw-redirect" title="Seabird colony">colonies</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jouventin&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jouventin-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Their eyes are adapted for underwater vision and are their primary means of locating prey and avoiding predators; in air it has been suggested that they are <a href="/wiki/Nearsighted" class="mw-redirect" title="Nearsighted">nearsighted</a>, although research has not supported this hypothesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sivak&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sivak-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pygoscelis_papua_-Nagasaki_Penguin_Aquarium_-swimming_underwater-8a.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Pygoscelis_papua_-Nagasaki_Penguin_Aquarium_-swimming_underwater-8a.jpg/250px-Pygoscelis_papua_-Nagasaki_Penguin_Aquarium_-swimming_underwater-8a.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Pygoscelis_papua_-Nagasaki_Penguin_Aquarium_-swimming_underwater-8a.jpg/500px-Pygoscelis_papua_-Nagasaki_Penguin_Aquarium_-swimming_underwater-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Gentoo_penguin" title="Gentoo penguin">Gentoo penguin</a> swimming underwater at the Nagasaki Penguin Aquarium</figcaption></figure>
<p>Penguins have a thick layer of insulating feathers that keeps them warm in water (heat loss in water is much greater than in air). The <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguin</a> has a maximum feather density of about nine feathers per square centimeter which is actually much lower than other birds that live in Antarctic environments. However, they have been identified as having at least four different <i>types</i> of feather: in addition to the traditional feather, the emperor has <a href="/wiki/Afterfeather" class="mw-redirect" title="Afterfeather">afterfeathers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">plumules</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Filoplume" class="mw-redirect" title="Filoplume">filoplumes</a>. The afterfeathers are downy plumes that attach directly to the main feathers and were once thought to account for the bird's ability to conserve heat when under water; the plumules are small down feathers that attach directly to the skin, and are much more dense in penguins than other birds; lastly the filoplumes are small (less than 1&#160;cm long) naked shafts that end in a splay of fibers— filoplumes were thought to give flying birds a sense of where their plumage was and whether or not it needed preening, so their presence in penguins may seem inconsistent, but penguins also preen extensively.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The emperor penguin has the largest body mass of all penguins, which further reduces relative surface area and heat loss. They also are able to control blood flow to their extremities, reducing the amount of blood that gets cold, but still keeping the extremities from freezing. In the extreme cold of the Antarctic winter, the females are at sea fishing for food, leaving the males to brave the weather by themselves. They often huddle together to keep warm and rotate positions to make sure that each penguin gets a turn in the centre of the heat pack.
</p><p>Calculations of the heat loss and retention ability of marine endotherms<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Calculating&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calculating-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> suggest that most extant penguins are too small to survive in such cold environments.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;penguins&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_penguins-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2007, Thomas and Fordyce wrote about the "heterothermic loophole" that penguins utilize in order to survive in Antarctica.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;heterothermic&#95;loophole&#95;exploited&#95;by&#95;penguins&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_heterothermic_loophole_exploited_by_penguins-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> All extant penguins, even those that live in warmer climates, have a counter-current heat exchanger called the humeral plexus. The flippers of penguins have at least three branches of the axillary artery, which allows cold blood to be heated by blood that has already been warmed and limits heat loss from the flippers. This system allows penguins to efficiently use their body heat and explains why such small animals can survive in the extreme cold.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Biological&#95;plasticity&#95;in&#95;penguin&#95;heat-retention&#95;structures&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biological_plasticity_in_penguin_heat-retention_structures-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>They can drink salt water because their <a href="/wiki/Supraorbital_gland" class="mw-redirect" title="Supraorbital gland">supraorbital gland</a> filters excess salt from the bloodstream.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The salt is excreted in a concentrated fluid from the nasal passages.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Penguins_on_Gourdin_Island.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Penguins_on_Gourdin_Island.jpg/250px-Penguins_on_Gourdin_Island.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="163" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Penguins_on_Gourdin_Island.jpg 2x" data-file-width="418" data-file-height="272" /></a><figcaption>An isabelline <a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lie_penguin" title="Adélie penguin">Adélie penguin</a> on <a href="/wiki/Gourdin_Island" title="Gourdin Island">Gourdin Island</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Around one in 50,000 penguins (of most species) are born with brown rather than black plumage. These are called isabelline penguins. Isabellinism is different from albinism. Isabelline penguins tend to live shorter lives than normal penguins, as they are not well-camouflaged against the deep and are often passed over as mates.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Behaviour">Behaviour</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv/250px--Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="188" data-durationhint="49" data-mwtitle="Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&quot;theora&quot;" data-width="384" data-height="288" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/75/Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv/Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="192" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/75/Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv/Pygoscelis_antarctica_trying_to_get_to_iceberg.wmv.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" /></video></span><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Chinstrap_penguin" title="Chinstrap penguin">Chinstrap penguins</a> in <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Breeding">Breeding</h3></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 222px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width: 220px; height: 250px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -50px; left: -110px; width: 450px"><div class="noresize"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Brown_Bluff-2016-Tabarin_Peninsula%E2%80%93Gentoo_penguin_(Pygoscelis_papua)_05.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Brown_Bluff-2016-Tabarin_Peninsula%E2%80%93Gentoo_penguin_%28Pygoscelis_papua%29_05.jpg/500px-Brown_Bluff-2016-Tabarin_Peninsula%E2%80%93Gentoo_penguin_%28Pygoscelis_papua%29_05.jpg" decoding="async" width="450" height="300" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Brown_Bluff-2016-Tabarin_Peninsula%E2%80%93Gentoo_penguin_%28Pygoscelis_papua%29_05.jpg/960px-Brown_Bluff-2016-Tabarin_Peninsula%E2%80%93Gentoo_penguin_%28Pygoscelis_papua%29_05.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4663" data-file-height="3108" /></a></span></div></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brown_Bluff-2016-Tabarin_Peninsula%E2%80%93Gentoo_penguin_(Pygoscelis_papua)_05.jpg" title="File:Brown Bluff-2016-Tabarin Peninsula–Gentoo penguin (Pygoscelis papua) 05.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Gentoo_penguin" title="Gentoo penguin">Gentoo penguin</a> watching over a sleeping chick at <a href="/wiki/Brown_Bluff" title="Brown Bluff">Brown Bluff</a>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Penguins for the most part breed in large colonies, the exceptions being the yellow-eyed and Fiordland species; these colonies may range in size from as few as 100 pairs for gentoo penguins to several hundred thousand in the case of king, macaroni and chinstrap penguins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Will17&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will17-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Living in colonies results in a high level of social interaction between birds, which has led to a large repertoire of visual as well as vocal displays in all penguin species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Williams57&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams57-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Agonistic</i> displays are those intended to confront or drive off, or alternately appease and avoid conflict with, other individuals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Williams57&#95;64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams57-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Penguins form monogamous pairs for a breeding season, though the rate the same pair recouples varies drastically. Most penguins lay two eggs in a clutch, although the two largest species, the emperor and the <a href="/wiki/King_penguin" title="King penguin">king penguins</a>, lay only one.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Will23&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will23-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the exception of the emperor penguin, where the male does it all, all penguins share the <a href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian incubation">incubation</a> duties.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These incubation shifts can last days and even weeks as one member of the pair feeds at sea.
</p><p>Penguins generally only lay one brood per year; the exception is the little penguin, which can raise two or three broods in a season.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Penguin eggs are smaller than any other bird species when compared proportionally to the weight of the parent birds; at 52&#160;g (2&#160;oz), the little penguin egg is 4.7% of its mothers' weight, and the 450&#160;g (1&#160;lb) emperor penguin egg is 2.3%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Will23&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will23-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The relatively thick shell forms between 10 and 16% of the weight of a penguin egg, presumably to reduce the effects of dehydration and to minimize the risk of breakage in an adverse nesting environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The yolk, too, is large and comprises 22–31% of the egg. Some yolk often remains when a chick is born, and is thought to help sustain the chick if the parents are delayed in returning with food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Will24&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Will24-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>When emperor penguin mothers lose a chick, they sometimes attempt to "steal" another mother's chick, usually unsuccessfully as other females in the vicinity assist the defending mother in keeping her chick.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In some species, such as emperor and king penguins, the chicks assemble in large groups called <a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A8che_(zoology)" title="Crèche (zoology)">crèches</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sphenisciformes_by_population" title="List of Sphenisciformes by population">List of Sphenisciformes by population</a></div>
<p>Although almost all penguin species are native to the Southern Hemisphere, they are not found only in cold climates, such as <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. In fact, only a few species of penguin actually live so far south. Several species live in the <a href="/wiki/Temperate" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate">temperate</a> zone;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (November 2020)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> one, the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_penguin" class="mw-redirect" title="Galápagos penguin">Galápagos penguin</a>, lives as far north as the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Galápagos Islands">Galápagos Islands</a>, but this is only made possible by the cold, rich waters of the Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_Current" title="Humboldt Current">Humboldt Current</a> that flows around these islands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Also, though the climate of the Arctic and Antarctic regions is similar, there are no penguins found in the Arctic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:G%C3%A1lapagos_Penguins_Near_Isabela_Island.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/G%C3%A1lapagos_Penguins_Near_Isabela_Island.jpg/250px-G%C3%A1lapagos_Penguins_Near_Isabela_Island.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/G%C3%A1lapagos_Penguins_Near_Isabela_Island.jpg/500px-G%C3%A1lapagos_Penguins_Near_Isabela_Island.jpg 2x" data-file-width="883" data-file-height="590" /></a><figcaption>Gálapagos Penguins near Isabela Island</figcaption></figure>
<p>Several authors have suggested that penguins are a good example of <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Bergmann&#39;s Rule">Bergmann's Rule</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where larger-bodied populations live at higher latitudes than smaller-bodied populations. There is some disagreement about this and several other authors have noted that there are fossil penguin species that contradict this hypothesis and that ocean currents and upwellings are likely to have had a greater effect on species diversity than latitude alone.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Major populations of penguins are found in Angola, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, Argentina, Australia, Chile, Namibia, New Zealand, and South Africa.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Satellite images and photos released in 2018 show the population of 2 million in France's remote <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele_aux_Cochons" title="Île aux Cochons">Ile aux Cochons</a> has collapsed, with barely 200,000 remaining, according to a study published in Antarctic Science.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Conservation_status">Conservation status</h3></div>
<p>The majority of living penguin species have declining populations. According to the IUCN Red List, their conservation statuses range from Least Concern through to Endangered.
</p>
