<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Penguins_and_humans">Penguins and humans</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaSummer.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/250px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/500px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1417" data-file-height="1063" /></a><figcaption>An <a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lie_penguin" title="Adélie penguin">Adélie penguin</a> (center) encountering humans during the Antarctic summer</figcaption></figure>
<p>Penguins have no special fear of humans and will often approach groups of people. This is probably because penguins have no land predators in <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> or the nearby offshore islands. They are preyed upon by other birds like skuas, especially in eggs and as fledglings. Other birds like petrels, sheathbills, and gulls also eat the chicks. Dogs preyed upon penguins while they were allowed in Antarctica during the age of early human exploration as <a href="/wiki/Sled_dog" title="Sled dog">sled dogs</a>, but dogs have long since been banned from Antarctica.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Instead, adult penguins are at risk at sea from predators such as sharks, <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">leopard seals</a>. Typically, penguins do not approach closer than around 9 feet (2.7 meters), at which point they appear to become nervous.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2011, an emperor penguin came ashore on New Zealand's <a href="/wiki/Peka_Peka" title="Peka Peka">Peka Peka Beach</a>, 3,200 kilometres (2,000&#160;mi) off course on its journey to Antarctica.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nicknamed <a href="/wiki/Happy_Feet_(penguin)" title="Happy Feet (penguin)">Happy Feet</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Happy_Feet" title="Happy Feet">film of the same name</a>, it was suffering from heat exhaustion and had to undergo a number of operations to remove objects like driftwood and sand from its stomach.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Happy Feet was a media sensation, with extensive coverage on TV and the web, including a live stream that had thousands of views<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a visit from English actor <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Fry" title="Stephen Fry">Stephen Fry</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Once he had recovered, Happy Feet was released back into the water south of New Zealand.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_penguins" title="Cultural depictions of penguins">Cultural depictions of penguins</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tux.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tux.svg/250px-Tux.svg.png" decoding="async" width="190" height="225" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tux.svg/500px-Tux.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="607" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Tux_(mascot)" title="Tux (mascot)">Tux</a>, the mascot of the <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">Linux kernel</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Penguins are widely considered endearing for their unusually upright, waddling <a href="/wiki/Gait" title="Gait">gait</a>, swimming ability and (compared to other birds) lack of fear of humans. Their black-and-white plumage is often likened to a <a href="/wiki/White_tie" title="White tie">white tie</a> suit. Some writers and artists have penguins based at the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>, but there are no wild penguins in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>. The cartoon series <i><a href="/wiki/Chilly_Willy" title="Chilly Willy">Chilly Willy</a></i> helped perpetuate this myth, as the title penguin would interact with Arctic or <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">sub-Arctic</a> species, such as <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bears</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walruses</a>.
</p><p>Penguins have been the subject of many books and films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Happy_Feet" title="Happy Feet">Happy Feet</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Surf%27s_Up_(film)" title="Surf&#39;s Up (film)">Surf's Up</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Penguins_of_Madagascar" title="Penguins of Madagascar">Penguins of Madagascar</a></i>, all <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">CGI</a> films; <i><a href="/wiki/March_of_the_Penguins" title="March of the Penguins">March of the Penguins</a></i>, a documentary based on the <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migration</a> process of the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguin</a>; and <i><a href="/wiki/Farce_of_the_Penguins" title="Farce of the Penguins">Farce of the Penguins</a></i>, a parody of the documentary. <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Popper%27s_Penguins" title="Mr. Popper&#39;s Penguins">Mr. Popper's Penguins</a></i> is a children's book written by Richard and Florence Atwater; it was named a <a href="/wiki/Newbery_Honor_Book" class="mw-redirect" title="Newbery Honor Book">Newbery Honor Book</a> in 1939. Penguins have also appeared in a number of cartoons and television dramas, including <i><a href="/wiki/Pingu" title="Pingu">Pingu</a></i>, co-created by <a href="/wiki/Otmar_Gutmann" title="Otmar Gutmann">Otmar Gutmann</a> and Erika Brueggemann in 1990 and covering more than 100 short episodes. At the end of 2009, <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> put it on its end-of-the-decade "best-of" list, saying, "Whether they were walking (<i>March of the Penguins</i>), dancing (<i>Happy Feet</i>), or hanging ten (<i>Surf's Up</i>), these oddly adorable birds took flight at the box office all decade long."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A video game called <i><a href="/wiki/Pengo_(video_game)" title="Pengo (video game)">Pengo</a></i> was released by <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega</a> in 1982. Set in Antarctica, the player controls a penguin character who must navigate mazes of ice cubes. The player is rewarded with cut-scenes of animated penguins marching, dancing, saluting and playing <a href="/wiki/Peekaboo" title="Peekaboo">peekaboo</a>. Several remakes and enhanced editions have followed, most recently in 2012. Penguins are also sometimes depicted in music.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1941, <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a> introduced the avian-themed character of the <a href="/wiki/Penguin_(character)" title="Penguin (character)">Penguin</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Supervillain" title="Supervillain">supervillain</a> adversary of the <a href="/wiki/Superhero" title="Superhero">superhero</a> <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a> (<i>Detective Comics</i> #58). He became one of the most enduring enemies in <a href="/wiki/List_of_Batman_family_enemies#Classic_rogues_gallery" title="List of Batman family enemies">Batman's rogues gallery</a>. In the 60s <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(TV_series)" title="Batman (TV series)">Batman</a></i> TV series, as played by <a href="/wiki/Burgess_Meredith" title="Burgess Meredith">Burgess Meredith</a>, he was one of the most popular characters, and in <a href="/wiki/Tim_Burton" title="Tim Burton">Tim Burton</a>'s reimagining of the story, the character played by <a href="/wiki/Danny_Devito" class="mw-redirect" title="Danny Devito">Danny Devito</a> in the 1992 film <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_Returns" title="Batman Returns">Batman Returns</a></i>, employed an actual army of penguins (mostly <a href="/wiki/African_penguin" title="African penguin">African penguins</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_penguin" title="King penguin">king penguins</a>).
</p><p>Several pro, minor, college and high school sport teams in the United States have named themselves after the species, including the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins" title="Pittsburgh Penguins">Pittsburgh Penguins</a> team in the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Youngstown_State_Penguins" title="Youngstown State Penguins">Youngstown State Penguins</a> in <a href="/wiki/College_athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="College athletics">college athletics</a>.
</p><p>Penguins featured regularly in the cartoons of U.K. cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bell_(cartoonist)" title="Steve Bell (cartoonist)">Steve Bell</a> in his strip in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> newspaper, particularly during and following the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>. <a href="/wiki/Opus_the_Penguin" title="Opus the Penguin">Opus the Penguin</a>, from the cartoons of <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Breathed" title="Berkeley Breathed">Berkeley Breathed</a>, is also described as hailing from the Falklands. Opus was a comical, "existentialist" penguin character in the cartoons <i><a href="/wiki/Bloom_County" title="Bloom County">Bloom County</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Outland_(comic_strip)" title="Outland (comic strip)">Outland</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_(comic_strip)" title="Opus (comic strip)">Opus</a></i>. He was also the star in the animated Christmas TV special <i><a href="/wiki/A_Wish_for_Wings_That_Work" title="A Wish for Wings That Work">A Wish for Wings That Work</a></i>.
</p><p>In the mid-2000s, penguins became one of the most publicized species of animals that form lasting <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality_in_animals#Penguins" class="mw-redirect" title="Homosexuality in animals">homosexual couples</a>. A children's book, <i><a href="/wiki/And_Tango_Makes_Three" title="And Tango Makes Three">And Tango Makes Three</a></i>, was written about <a href="/wiki/Roy_and_Silo" title="Roy and Silo">one such penguin family</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Park_Zoo" title="Central Park Zoo">New York Zoo</a>.
</p>

<li id="cite&#95;note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFHarry&#95;Baker2023" class="citation web cs1">Harry Baker (February 8, 2023). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.livescience.com/largest-penguin-ever-discovered-weighed-a-whopping-340-pounds-fossils-reveal">"Largest penguin ever discovered weighed a whopping 340 pounds, fossils reveal"</a>. <i>livescience.com</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite&#95;note-CB08-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CB08_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFChristidisBoles2008" class="citation book cs1">Christidis L, Boles WE (2008). <i>Systematics and Taxonomy of Australian Birds</i>. Canberra: CSIRO Publishing. p.&#160;97. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-643-06511-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-643-06511-6"><bdi>978-0-643-06511-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Systematics+and+Taxonomy+of+Australian+Birds&amp;rft.place=Canberra&amp;rft.pages=97&amp;rft.pub=CSIRO+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-643-06511-6&amp;rft.aulast=Christidis&amp;rft.aufirst=L&amp;rft.au=Boles%2C+WE&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APenguin" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
It is likely that, during the Bartonian, there was a near-synchronous but <a href="/wiki/Allopatric" class="mw-redirect" title="Allopatric">allopatric</a> split between the ancestors of <i>Aptenodytes</i>, <i>Pygoscelis</i>, and the common ancestor of all remaining genera (<a href="#Baker">Baker</a>).</span>
</li>
